  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16029685" />
    <a title="Sarah Clarkson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/721515-sarah-clarkson"><img alt="Sarah Clarkson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530559461p2/721515.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/16029685?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah Clarkson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah Clarkson" href="/user/show/721515-sarah-clarkson">Sarah Clarkson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16029685" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13370221625964142616">I always love Dickens. This is my sixth by him. I am always left a little breathless by the wit with which he sketches his characters. This book certainly had its unforgettable characters, my favorites were Cap'n Cuttle, Walter Gay, Mr. Toots and Susan, and for villain, the sheer toothiness of Mr. Carker is downright awful.<br/><br/>I found the book a bit slow in its first half though the gradual build of Florence and little Paul's relationship, especially down at the sea was pivotal to the whole story an</span>
  <span id="freeText13370221625964142616" style="display:none">I always love Dickens. This is my sixth by him. I am always left a little breathless by the wit with which he sketches his characters. This book certainly had its unforgettable characters, my favorites were Cap'n Cuttle, Walter Gay, Mr. Toots and Susan, and for villain, the sheer toothiness of Mr. Carker is downright awful.<br /><br />I found the book a bit slow in its first half though the gradual build of Florence and little Paul's relationship, especially down at the sea was pivotal to the whole story and very beautiful. Things really picked up with Mr. Dombey's second marriage, and from there, it was a pretty engrossing read. <br /><br />One of the themes seems to me to be extreme loyalty in this book: Florence's to her father, Walter's and Mr. Toot's and Susan's to Florence, Cap'n Cuttle to Walter and Sol Gills. I love the faithfulness of the good hearts in this tale. So, while this isn't my favorite Dickens, I loved it, left feeling enriched.<br /><br />I didn't find myself quite as connected to the characters as I had in other stories by him. With Our Mutual Friend and David Copperfield, I still feel as if they are friends that live in a different place. To read them again is to reconnect an old, familiar acquaintance. I never reached that depth with this book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13370221625964142616" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1813656413" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16332498931976767586">This book reminds me of a daytime soap opera. People arrive on the scene, die unexpectedly and dramatically disappear only to make an even more dramatic timely re-entrance. Dramatic co-incidences abound and are essential to the plot. Maybe this is as a direct result of its original serialisation by Dickens?<br/><br/>Overall, it’s a fun read. I found it easier to listen to on audio (due to Dickens long sentences) and the narrator (David Timson) was so good that I recognised voices of returning characters w</span>
  <span id="freeText16332498931976767586" style="display:none">This book reminds me of a daytime soap opera. People arrive on the scene, die unexpectedly and dramatically disappear only to make an even more dramatic timely re-entrance. Dramatic co-incidences abound and are essential to the plot. Maybe this is as a direct result of its original serialisation by Dickens?<br /><br />Overall, it’s a fun read. I found it easier to listen to on audio (due to Dickens long sentences) and the narrator (David Timson) was so good that I recognised voices of returning characters which were supposed to have a dramatic reveal later in the book. This book has a very large cast of characters so that was no insignificant achievement. I was having syncing problems with the book and ended up rereading huge chunks of this already chunky book but loved them too much to bother fast forwarding.<br /><br />Overall this is recommended for those who like a good Soap Opera written by a very talented classical author.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16332498931976767586" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Viv by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/28499942-julie-gilbert">Julie Gilbert</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4243437-viv-jm?shelf=z-2017-author-male">z-2017-author-male</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4243437-viv-jm?shelf=fiction-classic">fiction-classic</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1773171420" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11099708944261565817">3.5 stars, rounded up<br/><br/>Dombey and Son could be summed up with the adage: pride comes before a fall. It tells the tale of the downfall of the proud and arrogant Mr Dombey, who is <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ only redeemed after losing his family, his business and his health  <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. At times I found the book something of a slog, but there were also scenes that I enjoyed immensely, and scenes that had great emotional impact. Dickens imbues his characters with such distinct characteristics that they are </span>
  <span id="freeText11099708944261565817" style="display:none">3.5 stars, rounded up<br /><br />Dombey and Son could be summed up with the adage: pride comes before a fall. It tells the tale of the downfall of the proud and arrogant Mr Dombey, who is <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ only redeemed after losing his family, his business and his health  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. At times I found the book something of a slog, but there were also scenes that I enjoyed immensely, and scenes that had great emotional impact. Dickens imbues his characters with such distinct characteristics that they are very memorable - who can forget the wonderful Captain Cuttle, the comical blue-faced Major Bagstock with his propensity to refer to himself in the third person, and the villainous Mr Carker with his gleaming teeth (almost another character in themselves!)? I also love the way Dickens brought all the threads together at the end so nothing is left hanging.<br /><br />I listened to the Naxos Audiobook version of this, narrated by David Timson, who really was superb. He gave each character a distinct voice so that they were instantly recognisable. The only slightly unsettling thing was that Mrs Brown sounded just like my late paternal grandfather!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="11099708944261565817" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/588536928?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sherwood Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sherwood Smith" href="/user/show/162084-sherwood-smith">Sherwood Smith</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162084-sherwood-smith?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer588536928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer746649063689252375">Reading Dickens always reminds me that there was no such thing as an editor as we understand the function of an editor now. Dickens did carefully plot out his books — we have the evidence not only in letters but of his actual outline of how carefully this one was worked out. We can see through his letters where he deviated and where he stuck to the plot.<br/><br/> This is the first of his books that features a heroine rather than a hero at the center of the story. Florence, an unwanted daughter, is beneat</span>
  <span id="freeText746649063689252375" style="display:none">Reading Dickens always reminds me that there was no such thing as an editor as we understand the function of an editor now. Dickens did carefully plot out his books — we have the evidence not only in letters but of his actual outline of how carefully this one was worked out. We can see through his letters where he deviated and where he stuck to the plot.<br /><br /> This is the first of his books that features a heroine rather than a hero at the center of the story. Florence, an unwanted daughter, is beneath her father’s notice. He ignores her lest he come to hate her (which he will do) in his disappointment over not having a son. When he gets the son, he is so elated that he scarcely notices the death of his wife.<br /><br />Dombey's household, as well as his plans, are then organized around his little son Paul, who unfortunately is sickly. I think some of the best writing in the book is from Paul’s point of view, particularly when he is six years old. Carker's run was also amazing.<br /><br />Strong but at times somewhat ham-handed are the descriptions of the train-building tearing up the English city and countryside: the first is the best, comparing it to an earthquake that has devastated a poor section of town. Equally interesting is the vivid depictions of a cram school and of who does the work when a grand wedding is set up, when a household goes into bankruptcy, and what goes on in the servants' part of the house. <br /><br />I had some problems, as often happens with me and Dickens, with characterizations who are too obviously either Types to Make A Point, or caricatures. That's not to say they are all either Types or Caricatures. There are some brilliant characterizations, though exasperatingly enough, not all the main ones.<br /><br />Mrs. Miff, who was only on stage for a few paragraphs here and there, was more interesting (and believable) than Florence, who is just too good and pure and deferential to be believed. Another great character was Miss Tox, the aging spinster who begins as a comic character as she secretly nurses her hopes of attaching the widower, encouraged (or so she believes) by Mrs. Chick, Dombey’s horrible sister, but attains dignity and greatness of heart.<br /><br /> Dickens is so good with comic secondary characters! Mr. Tootle, whose healthy progeny Dombey envies; Sol Gill; JB Bagshot, Miss Tox, Capt. Cuttle, Mr. Toots, and Susan Nipper. Mrs. Pipchin and the Blimbers come close.<br /><br /> Dickens does something interesting with the second wife, Edith, demonstrating what happens to women whose only choice is to marry. At least, I think the idea was interesting, but Dickens drags the situation on to excruciating lengths, repeating the same points in long melodramatic scenes. The villain comes on stage softly, and could have been interesting, but Dickens tips his hand by having the good and pure characters repelled by him by instinct. If only truly evil people really did have a warning aura about them!<br /><br />I applaud Dickens for attempting the women's POV, but his heroines have no agency--all the more noticeable when I consider that the magnificent Becky Sharpe, in Thackeray's <i>Vanity Fair</i>, came out at exactly the same time.<br /><br />That reminds me: I enjoyed little glimpses of the past. In Dickens' eyes, the Regency period was raffish and tarnished at best, as we see in a risible character whose shoulder and back are exposed, at the dinner. (Dickens is not kind to older women dressing young, even, as this one did, in the fashions of her youth, vis. the Regency gown.) Actually, Thackeray does the same, in <i>Vanity Fair</i>, which is set around the time of the Regency, with the big set piece at Waterloo. Not a pleasant depiction at all!<br /><br /> All in all, a good read, which could have been a great read if the repetition, preaching, and near-death Victorian conversions had been tightened way, way down.</span>
  <a data-text-id="746649063689252375" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4355541761132609352">Pure Dickens, with all the good and the bad that statement invokes. The story sets up a couple I truly rooted for but gives neither of them a personality worth mentioning. The heroine is the angel of the house who pitifully pines after her emotionally distant Father's love and weepingly repents from her one act of backbone. The hero disappears for about 3/4ths of the book and his only claim to fame is that he manages to escape the brother-zone. <br/>The side-characters push the story forward. They br</span>
  <span id="freeText4355541761132609352" style="display:none">Pure Dickens, with all the good and the bad that statement invokes. The story sets up a couple I truly rooted for but gives neither of them a personality worth mentioning. The heroine is the angel of the house who pitifully pines after her emotionally distant Father's love and weepingly repents from her one act of backbone. The hero disappears for about 3/4ths of the book and his only claim to fame is that he manages to escape the brother-zone. <br />The side-characters push the story forward. They bring the humor, the memorablness, the zany lines. And they also make the novel atrociously long. It is an 880 pages that could have been told in a quarter of the space. Entire side-plots could easily have been cut out without losing much.<br />But then, if that happened, it wouldn't be Dickens, would it? <br />There is some obvious--and in my opinion, intriguing--subtext about gender and questioning the importance of having a son to carry on the male line. I'm still grappling with how the ending either upholds or subverts any message Dickens was trying to hint at. I'd be curious if there was much academic writing on this one.<br />This isn't my favorite and I wouldn't recommend starting here if you don't already love his writing, but it is definitely worth a read if you like Dickens.</span>

<div id="review_1247596975" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1247596975" />
    <a title="Julie Davis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2125014-julie-davis"><img alt="Julie Davis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277820647p2/2125014.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1247596975?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Davis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Davis" href="/user/show/2125014-julie-davis">Julie Davis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1247596975" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15231538488671092142">For a good, free audiobook try LibriVox's Mil Nicholson. She does some of the best voices I've ever heard although I don't enjoy her straight reading of the rest of the text quite as much.<br/><br/>I plumped for David Timson's reading which has some of the best expressive reading of the plain text I've heard, without being at all over the top about it.<br/><br/>======<br/><br/>FINAL<br/><br/><i>(Writing this without spoilers probably will lead to misdirection, but I feel there are too many people who probably haven't read this book. And </i></span>
  <span id="freeText15231538488671092142" style="display:none">For a good, free audiobook try LibriVox's Mil Nicholson. She does some of the best voices I've ever heard although I don't enjoy her straight reading of the rest of the text quite as much.<br /><br />I plumped for David Timson's reading which has some of the best expressive reading of the plain text I've heard, without being at all over the top about it.<br /><br />======<br /><br />FINAL<br /><br /><i>(Writing this without spoilers probably will lead to misdirection, but I feel there are too many people who probably haven't read this book. And I DO want to say some things.)</i><br /><br />*big sigh* Oh, that was good.<br /><br />It is interesting to me that I began reading Dickens with his later books. Going backwards to his earlier creations, one can see the training wheels on in some places. There are some very predictable plot developments that the reader sees as soon as the seeds are introduced. However, Dickens (that genius) still managed to surprise me again and again with unexpected twists that made the story lively and interesting.<br /><br />One thing that doesn't change from book to book is the creation of eccentric characters who begin by seeming odd and funny but wind up stealing your heart. I'd never have thought that I'd care passionately about Susan Nipper, Cap'n Cuttle, Miss Tox, or Mr. Toots but I really did. <br /><br />I also appreciated the way that Florence, the character with the least development in many ways (one motivation and one only) was used to show us so much about other characters. Mr. Dombey, Edith, and little Paul all showed surprising depth as they came into contact with Florence whose only desire was love. I was especially impressed with what we were shown of Mr. Dombey's internal character using this technique. <br /><br />There were times also when the power of Dickens' writing washed over me and left me bereft of my own words. Most notably in Mr. Dombey and Edith's argument in her bedroom, in Mr. Carker's conversation with Edith discussing Mr. Dombey's character, and in the chapter Rob the Grinder Loses His Place where I felt as tired and mentally confused as the fleeing fugitive whose thoughts we read. Here Dickens worked the seemingly impossible feat of making me sorry for someone who I'd been longing to see punished. <br /><br />On a personal note, I was astounded by in the chapter After a Lapse when Harriet is advancing a proposition to the fellow who plays the violoncello. This book suddenly became a reflection of how many times we stubbornly think we know best and refuse God's love, only to find that his forgiveness and mercy are boundless ... especially when we've fallen the very lowest and don't deserve it ... and yet it is freely and lovingly given. I don't know if that is what Dickens intended, but it is certainly what struck me hard. It was a revelatory moment that left me turning off the audiobook to simply think about the implications of that interpretation when applied to the other stories woven into the novel.<br /><br />It isn't the perfect Dickens novel. It sagged in the middle when there was a transition from emphasis on Paul to introducing Edith, and there were those predictable plot points I mentioned. It probably won't ever be my favorite (right now those honors are shared by The Pickwick Papers, Bleak House, and Little Dorrit), but it is a great book and very rewarding on a lot of levels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15231538488671092142" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_100008638" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100008638" />
    <a title="Duffy Pratt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2729963-duffy-pratt"><img alt="Duffy Pratt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1282095213p2/2729963.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/100008638?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Duffy Pratt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Duffy Pratt" href="/user/show/2729963-duffy-pratt">Duffy Pratt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2729963-duffy-pratt?shelf=classic">classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer100008638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer802956231417077585">This one is 800+ pages, and has about 300 pages of material in it. Worse, it almost feels like Dickens stepped back, analyzed his previous stuff, and decided that the problem with all of it was that there was too much fun. So he deliberately went out of his way to excise the fun, and accentuate the grim. Of course, being Dickens, he couldn't get rid of all the fun, and there are still some very charming moments here. But they are fairly few, and spread out sparsely over the course of what otherw</span>
  <span id="freeText802956231417077585" style="display:none">This one is 800+ pages, and has about 300 pages of material in it. Worse, it almost feels like Dickens stepped back, analyzed his previous stuff, and decided that the problem with all of it was that there was too much fun. So he deliberately went out of his way to excise the fun, and accentuate the grim. Of course, being Dickens, he couldn't get rid of all the fun, and there are still some very charming moments here. But they are fairly few, and spread out sparsely over the course of what otherwise amounts to a fairly tedious slog through some of the grimmest and dullest stuff he ever wrote.<br /><br />The main problem, in my eyes, is that the two main characters, Dombey and his daughter, are almost completely passive. Florence is slighted and unloved. What does she do about it? She perseveres. And that's about it, for 800 pages. Dombey is a great businessman, and he takes his entire identity from his business. But what does he do? I haven't a clue. Mostly, he seems to accept compliments on his greatness, and otherwise remains haughty and taciturn.<br /><br />There are a few interesting and charming characters here, notably Captain Cuttle and Toots. But, except possibly for Cuttle, they are all very much of the Dickens' one-note variety. And with some, it goes to new extremes. Carker, for example, seems to be nothing but a set of teeth.<br /><br />I like the basic set-up. Dombey, who identifies himself with his business (Dombey and Son), is left with an only daughter, whom he despises. But Florence, his daughter, is so perfect that she is a total snooze. And Dombey is so shallow that it's almost impossible to take an interest in him. Combine with that with the ultimate passivity of these two, and its amazing that the novel goes anywhere at all, even if it is at a glacial pace.<br /><br />I know this sounds like I hated the novel, and in some ways I did. But as always, with Dickens, there are passages and chapters that are so brilliant that they partially redeem what would otherwise be irredeemable. And that's also true here. And because of that, I will probably go on to read the remaining books that I haven't: Sketches by Boz, Old Curiosity Shop, and Our Mutual Friend. I'll probably do Old Curiosity next, and I expect it to be even worse. I've never heard anyone say anything good about Little Nell. And I'm leaving Mutual Friend for last, because I have hopes that it will be really good.</span>
  <a data-text-id="802956231417077585" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3196924533" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3196924533" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20238041-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1441408011p2/20238041.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3196924533?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/20238041-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781101488843" href="/book/show/11011045-dombey-and-son">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3196924533" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2958369326122959629">Some consider "Dombey and Son" the first of Dickens's later novels. It is a beautifully written, monumental work that this reader thoroughly enjoyed. Here, the underlying theme is pride and its terrible collateral effect on a family. To explore this, Dickens created one intensely dysfunctional family, the Dombeys. <br/><br/>As usual, there is a large cast of eccentric characters which surround the central characters. And, while Mr. Dickens certainly knows how to tug on a heart-string, there are also some </span>
  <span id="freeText2958369326122959629" style="display:none">Some consider "Dombey and Son" the first of Dickens's later novels. It is a beautifully written, monumental work that this reader thoroughly enjoyed. Here, the underlying theme is pride and its terrible collateral effect on a family. To explore this, Dickens created one intensely dysfunctional family, the Dombeys. <br /><br />As usual, there is a large cast of eccentric characters which surround the central characters. And, while Mr. Dickens certainly knows how to tug on a heart-string, there are also some riveting passages, including one bitterly intense argument between a husband and wife.<br /><br />It is hard for this reader to rank all of the Dickens novels. I certainly prefer the later ones and would place "Dombey and Son" in my top 5 group. Cheers!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2958369326122959629" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3193412183" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3193412183" />
    <a title="Ruthiella" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3082480-ruthiella"><img alt="Ruthiella" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1273515825p2/3082480.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3193412183?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ruthiella" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ruthiella" href="/user/show/3082480-ruthiella">Ruthiella</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140435467" href="/book/show/400887.Dombey_and_Son">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3082480-ruthiella?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3082480-ruthiella?shelf=2020-backtotheclassics">2020-backtotheclassics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3193412183" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8709132295908060053">Paul Dombey is a Scrooge like character. However, his is not miserly with his money; it is his love and affection that he withholds from all others, with the exception of his son and heir whom he expects to take over the business of Dombey and Son one day. This imbalance not only affects his young son, it also affects his older daughter, Florence who seeks her father’s love the way a plant seeks the nourishing rays of the sun. This is the story of a dysfunctional family in the extreme. Their emo</span>
  <span id="freeText8709132295908060053" style="display:none">Paul Dombey is a Scrooge like character. However, his is not miserly with his money; it is his love and affection that he withholds from all others, with the exception of his son and heir whom he expects to take over the business of Dombey and Son one day. This imbalance not only affects his young son, it also affects his older daughter, Florence who seeks her father’s love the way a plant seeks the nourishing rays of the sun. This is the story of a dysfunctional family in the extreme. Their emotional impoverishment is only accentuated by the loving depiction of the Toodle family and the wonderful ersatz father/son relationship between Soll Gills and his nephew Walter Gay. <br /><br />This really was a lovely book to end up on since it is the last book I had to read of all of Dickens’ completed novels! I think Dickens, who really loves a deathbed scene, wrote two very fine ones in this tome. And as all in Dickens’ novels, it is replete with humor. I laughed out loud more than once. <br /><br />I did find that Florence does occasionally edged on Little Nell-like saccharine devotion and she is, as most of Dickens’ heroines are, a cipher whose one personality trait is saintliness. However, it grated less in this novel because Florence has been so ill-treated psychologically from childhood; I can believe she would develop a masochistic like devotion to the hand that bites her. But aside from the tragic and touching main plot, the side characters are so ouststanding! Cap’n Cuttle (“Stand by!”), Mr. Toots (“It’s of no consequence”), Miss Nipper, Miss Tox,… they are all amazingly well constructed and used in the plot and development of the novel. Also, honorable mention goes to little Paul, that old-fashioned child. And the villains are equally wonderful, Major Blagstock (that de-vilish old soldier, J.B., Josh, Joey B., J. Bagstock, as observed by his royal highness, the late Duke of York on more than one occasion), the horrifically eternally youthful Mrs. Skewton, and Mr. Carker with all his intimidating white teeth. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8709132295908060053" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_132267913" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/132267913" />
    <a title="Jimyanni" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2858587-jimyanni"><img alt="Jimyanni" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1275506740p2/2858587.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/132267913?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jimyanni" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jimyanni" href="/user/show/2858587-jimyanni">Jimyanni</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140430486" href="/book/show/1275487.Dombey_and_Son">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2858587-jimyanni?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer132267913" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18085486988099054504">Dickens as feminist? Not by 21st century definitions, perhaps, but by the standards of his own time, definitely. In this book, he very clearly perceives, and shows to the reader, the wrongness of a father discounting the value of his daughter simply because she is not a son, as well as various other indications of the wrongness of the discriminations visited upon women in his Victorian England, and the innate strength of many of his female characters in dealing with what their life forces them t</span>
  <span id="freeText18085486988099054504" style="display:none">Dickens as feminist? Not by 21st century definitions, perhaps, but by the standards of his own time, definitely. In this book, he very clearly perceives, and shows to the reader, the wrongness of a father discounting the value of his daughter simply because she is not a son, as well as various other indications of the wrongness of the discriminations visited upon women in his Victorian England, and the innate strength of many of his female characters in dealing with what their life forces them to endure. Even if his characters are largely one-dimensional, and either idealized near-saints, perfect villains, or caricatures, they are still generally interesting, and even if he is more than a touch long-winded at times, his writing is still enjoyable. This is one of my favorites of his writing, perhaps exceeded only by "A Christmas Carol", and matched, perhaps, by "Our Mutual Friend".</span>
  <a data-text-id="18085486988099054504" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fakxFzk6pCRNIooLLnCGR+/aDuZ9wc4O/YxZ9KR83BtdfybNi0mncnFOCZD0vutpUeP7dFgNt4meUFKEBti7Dg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5BJ1Huzi3kKCc57gA+hXQnjd2nMvlJ5Z19JGT8p6xKHExGLEXpHdFL4fHXvZJjpsxuQv4QpY5960Dk0/aN6jtA==')})
  }

//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_31168'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31168.Shirley?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Shirley<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/1036615.Charlotte_Bront_?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Charlotte Brontë<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.75 avg rating &mdash; 30,430 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1849\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer16023265980093484755\">Following the tremendous popular success of Jane Eyre, which earned her lifelong notoriety as a moral revolutionary, Charlotte Brontë vowed to write a sweeping social chronicle that focused on \"something real and unromantic as Monday morning.\" Set in<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText16023265980093484755\" style=\"display:none\">Following the tremendous popular success of Jane Eyre, which earned her lifelong notoriety as a moral revolutionary, Charlotte Brontë vowed to write a sweeping social chronicle that focused on &quot;something real and unromantic as Monday morning.&quot; Set in the industrializing England of the Napoleonic wars and Luddite revolts of 1811-12, Shirley (1849) is the story of two contrasting heroines. One is the shy Caroline Helstone, who is trapped in the oppressive atmosphere of a Yorkshire rectory and whose bare life symbolizes the plight of single women in the nineteenth century. The other is the vivacious Shirley Keeldar, who inherits a local estate and whose wealth liberates her from convention.A work that combines social commentary with the more private preoccupations of Jane Eyre, Shirley demonstrates the full range of Brontë\'s literary talent. &quot;Shirley is a revolutionary novel,&quot; wrote Brontë biographer Lyndall Gordon. &quot;Shirley follows Jane Eyre as a new exemplar but so much a forerunner of the feminist of the later twentieth century that it is hard to believe in her actual existence in 1811-12. She is a theoretic possibility: what a woman might be if she combined independence and means of her own with intellect. Unlike the &quot;New Woman&quot; novels of the era which challenged the idea that the unmarried woman was superfluous, Gissing satirizes that image and portrays women as &quot;odd&quot; and marginal in relation to an ideal. Set in a grimy, fog-ridden London, Gissing\'s &quot;odd&quot; women range from the idealistic, financially self-sufficient Mary Barfoot to the Madden sisters who struggle to subsist in low paying jobs and little chance for joy.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens">About Charles Dickens</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1387078070p3/239579.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens">Charles Dickens
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
23,205
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15390356920927821195"><b>Charles John Huffam Dickens</b> was a writer and social critic who created some of the world's best-known fictional characters and is regarded as the greatest novelist of the Victorian era. His works enjoyed unprecedented popularity during his lifetime, and by the twentieth century critics and scholars had recognised him as a literary genius. His novels and short stories enjoy lasting popularity.<br/><br/>Dicke</span>
  <span id="freeText15390356920927821195" style="display:none"><b>Charles John Huffam Dickens</b> was a writer and social critic who created some of the world's best-known fictional characters and is regarded as the greatest novelist of the Victorian era. His works enjoyed unprecedented popularity during his lifetime, and by the twentieth century critics and scholars had recognised him as a literary genius. His novels and short stories enjoy lasting popularity.<br /><br />Dickens left school to work in a factory when his father was incarcerated in a debtors' prison. Despite his lack of formal education, he edited a weekly journal for 20 years, wrote 15 novels, five novellas, hundreds of short stories and non-fiction articles, lectured and performed extensively, was an indefatigable letter writer, and campaigned vigorously for children's rights, education, and other social reforms.<br /><br />Dickens was regarded as the literary colossus of his age. His 1843 novella, <i>A Christmas Carol</i>, remains popular and continues to inspire adaptations in every artistic genre. <i>Oliver Twist</i> and <i>Great Expectations</i> are also frequently adapted, and, like many of his novels, evoke images of early Victorian London. His 1859 novel, <i>A Tale of Two Cities</i>, set in London and Paris, is his best-known work of historical fiction. Dickens's creative genius has been praised by fellow writers—from Leo Tolstoy to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3706.George_Orwell" title="George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">George Orwell</a> and G. K. Chesterton—for its realism, comedy, prose style, unique characterisations, and social criticism. On the other hand, Oscar Wilde, Henry James, and Virginia Woolf complained of a lack of psychological depth, loose writing, and a vein of saccharine sentimentalism. The term Dickensian is used to describe something that is reminiscent of Dickens and his writings, such as poor social conditions or comically repulsive characters.<br /><br />On 8 June 1870, Dickens suffered another stroke at his home after a full day's work on <i>Edwin Drood.</i> He never regained consciousness, and the next day he died at Gad's Hill Place. Contrary to his wish to be buried at Rochester Cathedral "in an inexpensive, unostentatious, and strictly private manner," he was laid to rest in the Poets' Corner of Westminster Abbey. A printed epitaph circulated at the time of the funeral reads: "To the Memory of Charles Dickens (England's most popular author) who died at his residence, Higham, near Rochester, Kent, 9 June 1870, aged 58 years. He was a sympathiser with the poor, the suffering, and the oppressed; and by his death, one of England's greatest writers is lost to the world." His last words were: "On the ground", in response to his sister-in-law Georgina's request that he lie down.<br /><br />(from Wikipedia)</span>
  <a data-text-id="15390356920927821195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
